   </h1>

     <p>
      "
      <b>
       Instant Karma!
      </b>
      "  sometimes referred to as "
      <b>
       Instant Karma! (We All Shine On)
      </b>
      "  is a song written by English musician
      <a href="/wiki/John_Lennon" title="John Lennon">
       John Lennon
      </a>
      , released as a single on
      <a href="/wiki/Apple_Records" title="Apple Records">
       Apple Records
      </a>
      in February 1970. In the UK, the single was credited to "Lennon/
      <a href="/wiki/Yoko_Ono" title="Yoko Ono">
       Ono
      </a>
      with the
      <a href="/wiki/Plastic_Ono_Band" title="Plastic Ono Band">
       Plastic Ono Band
      </a>
      ". The song reached the top five in the British and American singles charts, competing with
      <a href="/wiki/The_Beatles" title="The Beatles">
       the Beatles
      </a>
      ' "
      <a href="/wiki/Let_It_Be_(song)" title="Let It Be (song)">
       Let It Be
      </a>
      " in America, where it became the first solo single by a member of the band to sell a million copies.
     </p>
     <p>
      "Instant Karma!" was written, recorded and released within a period of ten days, making it one of the fastest-released songs in pop music history. The recording was produced by
      <a href="/wiki/Phil_Spector" title="Phil Spector">
       Phil Spector
      </a>
      , marking a comeback for the American producer after his self-imposed retirement in 1966, and leading to him being offered the producer's role on the Beatles'
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Let_It_Be" title="Let It Be">
        Let It Be
       </a>
      </i>
      album (1970). Recorded at London's
      <a href="/wiki/Abbey_Road_Studios" title="Abbey Road Studios">
       Abbey Road Studios
      </a>
      , "Instant Karma!" employs Spector's signature
      <a href="/wiki/Wall_of_Sound" title="Wall of Sound">
       Wall of Sound
      </a>
      technique and features contributions from
      <a href="/wiki/George_Harrison" title="George Harrison">
       George Harrison
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Billy_Preston" title="Billy Preston">
       Billy Preston
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Klaus_Voormann" title="Klaus Voormann">
       Klaus Voormann
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Alan_White_(Yes_drummer)" title="Alan White (Yes drummer)">
       Alan White
      </a>
      . The
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/B-side" title="B-side">
       B-side
      </a>
      was a song composed and performed by
      <a href="/wiki/Yoko_Ono" title="Yoko Ono">
       Yoko Ono
      </a>
      , titled "Who Has Seen the Wind?" Recently shorn of the long hair synonymous with their
      <a href="/wiki/Bed-In" title="Bed-In">
       1969 campaign
      </a>
      for
      <a href="/wiki/Peace_movement" title="Peace movement">
       world peace
      </a>
      , Lennon and Ono promoted the single with an appearance on Britain's
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Top_of_the_Pops" title="Top of the Pops">
        Top of the Pops
       </a>
      </i>
      .
     </p>
     <p>
      "Instant Karma!" has appeared on many Lennon compilations, including
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Shaved_Fish" title="Shaved Fish">
        Shaved Fish
       </a>
      </i>
      (1975),
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Lennon_Legend:_The_Very_Best_of_John_Lennon" title="Lennon Legend: The Very Best of John Lennon">
        Lennon Legend
       </a>
      </i>
      (1997) and
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Power_to_the_People:_The_Hits" title="Power to the People: The Hits">
        Power to the People: The Hits
       </a>
      </i>
      (2010). A version recorded at the "One to One" concerts in August 1972 was included on his posthumously released
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Live_in_New_York_City_(John_Lennon_album)" title="Live in New York City (John Lennon album)">
        Live in New York City
       </a>
      </i>
      album (1986). The song continues to receive critical praise as one of the finest recordings from Lennon's solo career.
      <a href="/wiki/Paul_Weller" title="Paul Weller">
       Paul Weller
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Duran_Duran" title="Duran Duran">
       Duran Duran
      </a>
      , and
      <a href="/wiki/U2" title="U2">
       U2
      </a>
      are among the acts who have covered "Instant Karma!", the chorus of which inspired the title to
      <a href="/wiki/Stephen_King" title="Stephen King">
       Stephen King
      </a>
      's 1977 novel
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/The_Shining_(novel)" title="The Shining (novel)">
        The Shining
       </a>
      </i>
      .
     </p>
     <p>
     </p>

     <p>

     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Background">
       Background
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Instant_Karma!&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Background">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <p>
      Together with his wife,
      <a href="/wiki/Yoko_Ono" title="Yoko Ono">
       Yoko Ono
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/John_Lennon" title="John Lennon">
       John Lennon
      </a>
      spent New Year 1970 in
      <a href="/wiki/Aalborg" title="Aalborg">
       Aalborg
      </a>
      , Denmark,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-1">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        1
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      establishing a relationship with Ono's former husband, artist
      <a href="/wiki/Anthony_Cox_(producer)" title="Anthony Cox (producer)">
       Tony Cox
      </a>
      , and visiting Cox and Ono's daughter Kyoko.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Madinger_.26_Easter_p_33_2-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Madinger_.26_Easter_p_33-2">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        2
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The visit coincided with the start of what Lennon termed "Year 1 AP (After Peace)",
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3">
       <a href="#cite_note-3">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        3
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      following his and Ono's much-publicised
      <a href="/wiki/Bed-In" title="Bed-In">
       Bed-Ins
      </a>
      and other
      <a href="/wiki/World_peace" title="World peace">
       peace-campaign
      </a>
      activities throughout 1969.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4">
       <a href="#cite_note-4">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        4
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RS_bio_5-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-RS_bio-5">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        5
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      To mark the new era,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Woffinden_p_39_6-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Woffinden_p_39-6">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        6
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      on 20 January 1970, the couple shaved off their shoulder-length hair, an act that Britain's
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Daily_Mirror" title="Daily Mirror">
        Daily Mirror
       </a>
      </i>
      described as "the most sensational scalpings since the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Red_Indian" title="Red Indian">
       Red Indians
      </a>
      went out of business".
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Miles_p_367_7-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Miles_p_367-7">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        7
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Lennon and Ono pledged to auction the shorn hair for a charitable cause,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Doggett_p_118_8-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Doggett_p_118-8">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        8
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      having similarly announced on 5 January
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Madinger_.26_Easter_p_33_2-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Madinger_.26_Easter_p_33-2">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        2
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      that they would donate all future royalties from their recordings to the
      <a href="/wiki/Peace_movement" title="Peace movement">
       peace movement
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9">
       <a href="#cite_note-9">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        9
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Also while in Denmark, the Lennons, Cox and the latter's current partner, Melinde Kendall, discussed the concept of "instant
      <a href="/wiki/Karma" title="Karma">
       karma
      </a>
      ",
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Listen50_10-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Listen50-10">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        10
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      whereby the
      <a href="/wiki/Causality" title="Causality">
       causality
      </a>
      of one's actions is immediate rather than borne out over a lifetime.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11">
       <a href="#cite_note-11">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        11
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12">
       <a href="#cite_note-12">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        12
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Author
      <a href="/wiki/Philip_Norman_(author)" title="Philip Norman (author)">
       Philip Norman
      </a>
      writes of the concept's appeal: "The idea was quintessential Lennon  the age-old Buddhist law of cause and effect turned into something as modern and synthetic as instant coffee and, simultaneously, into a bogey under the stairs that can get you if you don't watch out."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Norman_p_635_13-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Norman_p_635-13">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        13
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Composition">
       Composition
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Instant_Karma!&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Composition">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <div class="quotebox" style="float: left; clear: left; margin: 0.5em 1.4em 0.8em 0; width:20%; padding: 6px; border: 1px solid #aaa; font-size: 88%; background-color: #F9F9F9; padding:8px;">
      <div class="" style="position: relative; text-align: left;">
       <div>
        <p>
         Everybody was going on about
         <a href="/wiki/Karma" title="Karma">
          karma
         </a>
          But it occurred to me that karma is instant as well as it influences your past life or your future life  I'm fascinated by commercials, as an art form  So the idea of instant karma was like the idea of instant coffee: presenting something in a new form."
         <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14">
          <a href="#cite_note-14">
           <span>
            [
           </span>
           14
           <span>
            ]
           </span>
          </a>
         </sup>
        </p>
       </div>
      </div>
      <div style="text-align: left;">
        John Lennon to
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Playboy" title="Playboy">
         Playboy
        </a>
       </i>
       magazine, 1980
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      On 27 January 1970, two days after returning to the UK,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Miles_p_367_7-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Miles_p_367-7">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        7
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Lennon woke up with the beginnings of a song inspired by his conversations with Cox and Kendall.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rodriguez_p_21_15-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Rodriguez_p_21-15">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        15
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Working at home on a piano, Lennon developed the idea and came up with a melody for the composition, which he titled "Instant Karma!"
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16">
       <a href="#cite_note-16">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        16
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AYNWL97_17-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-AYNWL97-17">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        17
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The song employs a similar
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chord_structure" title="Chord structure">
       chord structure
      </a>
      to that of "
      <a href="/wiki/Three_Blind_Mice" title="Three Blind Mice">
       Three Blind Mice
      </a>
      "
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Listen50_10-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Listen50-10">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        10
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      and "
      <a href="/wiki/Some_Other_Guy" title="Some Other Guy">
       Some Other Guy
      </a>
      ",
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18">
       <a href="#cite_note-18">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        18
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20">
       <a href="#cite_note-20">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        nb 1
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      after Lennon had used the same progression in his 1967 composition for
      <a href="/wiki/The_Beatles" title="The Beatles">
       the Beatles
      </a>
      , "
      <a href="/wiki/All_You_Need_Is_Love" title="All You Need Is Love">
       All You Need Is Love
      </a>
      ".
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JLBio133_21-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-JLBio133-21">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        20
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Later in 1970, he would adopt the melody of "Three Blind Mice", an English nursery rhyme, for his song "
      <a href="/wiki/My_Mummy%27s_Dead" title="My Mummy's Dead">
       My Mummy's Dead
      </a>
      ".
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22">
       <a href="#cite_note-22">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        21
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23">
       <a href="#cite_note-23">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        22
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      In their book
      <i>
       The Words and Music of John Lennon
      </i>
      , Ben Urish and Kenneth Bielen suggest that in the first verse of "Instant Karma!", Lennon rebukes his listeners with the sarcastic lines: "
      <i>
       Get yourself together / Pretty soon you're gonna be dead.
      </i>
      "
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-W.26MJL16_24-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-W.26MJL16-24">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        23
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Norman comments on the "hippie catchphrase of the moment" contained in the first of these two lines, which together provide a warning that is "obviously not to be taken literally".
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Norman_p_635_13-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Norman_p_635-13">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        13
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Author
      <a href="/wiki/Mark_Hertsgaard" title="Mark Hertsgaard">
       Mark Hertsgaard
      </a>
      notes the lyric "
      <i>
       Why in the world are we here?
      </i>
      " as a further example of Lennon "asking what purpose his life on earth was to serve", after his 1966 composition "
      <a href="/wiki/Strawberry_Fields_Forever" title="Strawberry Fields Forever">
       Strawberry Fields Forever
      </a>
      ".
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25">
       <a href="#cite_note-25">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        24
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      As with "
      <a href="/wiki/Give_Peace_a_Chance" title="Give Peace a Chance">
       Give Peace a Chance
      </a>
      " and "
      <a href="/wiki/Power_to_the_People_(song)" title="Power to the People (song)">
       Power to the People
      </a>
      "
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-W.26MJL25_26-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-W.26MJL25-26">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        25
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
       Lennon singles from 1969 and 1971 respectively  the chorus has an anthem-like quality, as he sings: "
      <i>
       We all shine on, like the moon and the stars and the sun.
      </i>
      "
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-W.26MJL16_24-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-W.26MJL16-24">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        23
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Norman describes the chorus as Lennon restating his message of "peace campaigning and non-violent, optimistic togetherness".
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Norman_p_635_13-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-Norman_p_635-13">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        13
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Lennon biographer John Blaney writes that the song is an appeal "for mankind to take responsibility for its fate" and that it was "Lennon developing his own brand of egalitarianism".
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Listen50_10-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-Listen50-10">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        10
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Lennon completed the writing of "Instant Karma!" in an hour.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Madinger_.26_Easter_p_33_2-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-Madinger_.26_Easter_p_33-2">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        2
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      He then telephoned bandmate
      <a href="/wiki/George_Harrison" title="George Harrison">
       George Harrison
      </a>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rodriguez_p_21_15-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Rodriguez_p_21-15">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        15
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      and American producer
      <a href="/wiki/Phil_Spector" title="Phil Spector">
       Phil Spector
      </a>
      ,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AYNWL97_17-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-AYNWL97-17">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        17
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      who was in London at the invitation of the Beatles'
      <a href="/wiki/Apple_Corps" title="Apple Corps">
       Apple Corps
      </a>
      manager,
      <a href="/wiki/Allen_Klein" title="Allen Klein">
       Allen Klein
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27">
       <a href="#cite_note-27">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        26
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Woffinden_p_31_28-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Woffinden_p_31-28">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        27
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      According to Lennon's recollection, he told Spector: "Come over to Apple quick, I've just written a monster."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AYNWL97_17-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-AYNWL97-17">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        17
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Recording">
       Recording
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Instant_Karma!&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Recording">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <p>
      Although still officially a member of the Beatles, Lennon had privately announced his departure from the group in September 1969.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29">
       <a href="#cite_note-29">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        28
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      He was now keen to issue "Instant Karma!" immediately as a single, the third under his and Ono's
      <a href="/wiki/Plastic_Ono_Band" title="Plastic Ono Band">
       Plastic Ono Band
      </a>
      moniker.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schaffner_p_137_30-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Schaffner_p_137-30">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        29
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The recording session took place at
      <a href="/wiki/Abbey_Road_Studios" title="Abbey Road Studios">
       Abbey Road Studios
      </a>
      in north-west London, on the evening of 27 January.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rodriguez_p_21_15-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-Rodriguez_p_21-15">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        15
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Lennon's fellow musicians at the session were Harrison,
      <a href="/wiki/Klaus_Voormann" title="Klaus Voormann">
       Klaus Voormann
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Alan_White_(Yes_drummer)" title="Alan White (Yes drummer)">
       Alan White
      </a>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ribowsky_p_251_31-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Ribowsky_p_251-31">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        30
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Billy_Preston" title="Billy Preston">
       Billy Preston
      </a>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Spizer_p_28_32-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Spizer_p_28-32">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        31
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
       all of whom had performed at the December 1969
      <a href="/wiki/Some_Time_in_New_York_City#Recording" title="Some Time in New York City">
       Peace for Christmas Concert
      </a>
      ,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33">
       <a href="#cite_note-33">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        32
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34">
       <a href="#cite_note-34">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        33
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      as part of the
      <a href="/wiki/Plastic_Ono_Band" title="Plastic Ono Band">
       Plastic Ono Supergroup
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35">
       <a href="#cite_note-35">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        34
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36">
       <a href="#cite_note-36">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        35
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The recording engineer for "Instant Karma!" was
      <a href="/wiki/EMI" title="EMI">
       EMI
      </a>
      mainstay
      <a href="/wiki/Phil_McDonald" title="Phil McDonald">
       Phil McDonald
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Spizer_p_28_32-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Spizer_p_28-32">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        31
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Spector produced the session,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Listen51_37-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Listen51-37">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        36
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      arriving late
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Williams_p_143_38-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Williams_p_143-38">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        37
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      after Harrison had found him at Apple's office and persuaded him to attend.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Doggett_p_115_39-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Doggett_p_115-39">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        38
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <div class="quotebox" style="float: right; clear: right; margin: 0.5em 0 0.8em 1.4em; width:20%; padding: 6px; border: 1px solid #aaa; font-size: 88%; background-color: #F9F9F9; padding:8px;">
      <div class="" style="position: relative; text-align: left;">
       <div>
        <p>
         [T]here was this little guy walking around with "PS" on his shirt, and I was thinking, "Who is this guy?"  When he turned on the playback [after recording], it was just incredible. First, it was ridiculously loud, but also there was the ring of all these instruments and the way the song had such motion. As a first experience of the difference from the way you played it to the sound in the control room, it was overwhelming. And I knew immediately who he was  Phil Spector.
         <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Leng_p_70_40-0">
          <a href="#cite_note-Leng_p_70-40">
           <span>
            [
           </span>
           39
           <span>
            ]
           </span>
          </a>
         </sup>
        </p>
       </div>
      </div>
      <div style="text-align: left;">
       
       <a href="/wiki/Klaus_Voormann" title="Klaus Voormann">
        Klaus Voormann
       </a>
       , describing his first experience of working with Spector and his
       <a href="/wiki/Wall_of_Sound" title="Wall of Sound">
        Wall of Sound
       </a>
       technique
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      The line-up for the basic track, before
      <a href="/wiki/Overdubbing" title="Overdubbing">
       overdubs
      </a>
      , was Lennon (vocals, acoustic guitar), Harrison (electric guitar), Preston (organ), Voormann (bass) and White (drums).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Spizer_p_28_32-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-Spizer_p_28-32">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        31
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Lennon later recalled of the recording: "Phil (Spector) came in and said, 'How do you want it?' And I said, '1950s' and he said 'Right' and BOOM! ... he played it back and there it was."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Listen50-51_41-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Listen50-51-41">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        40
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The song uses a similar amount of
      <a href="/wiki/Reverberation" title="Reverberation">
       echo
      </a>
      to 1950s
      <a href="/wiki/Sun_Records" title="Sun Records">
       Sun Records
      </a>
      recordings.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-W.26MJL16_24-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-W.26MJL16-24">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        23
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The musicians recorded ten takes,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Norman_p_635_13-3">
       <a href="#cite_note-Norman_p_635-13">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        13
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      the last of which was selected for overdubbing.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Madinger_.26_Easter_p_33_2-3">
       <a href="#cite_note-Madinger_.26_Easter_p_33-2">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        2
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      To create what Spector biographer Mark Ribowsky terms a "four-man
      <a href="/wiki/Wall_of_Sound" title="Wall of Sound">
       Wall of Sound
      </a>
      " production,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42">
       <a href="#cite_note-42">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        41
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Lennon added
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Grand_piano" title="Grand piano">
       grand piano
      </a>
      onto the basic track,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Williams_p_143_38-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Williams_p_143-38">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        37
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Carr_.26_Tyler_p_86_43-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Carr_.26_Tyler_p_86-43">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        42
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      while Harrison and White shared another piano and Voormann played electric piano.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Brown_p_242_44-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Brown_p_242-44">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        43
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47">
       <a href="#cite_note-47">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        nb 2
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      In addition, Beatles aide
      <a href="/wiki/Mal_Evans" title="Mal Evans">
       Mal Evans
      </a>
      overdubbed chimes (or
      <a href="/wiki/Tubular_bells" title="Tubular bells">
       tubular bells
      </a>
      )
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-C.26P_p_171_46-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-C.26P_p_171-46">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        45
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      and White added a second, muffled drum part.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ribowsky_p_252_45-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Ribowsky_p_252-45">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        44
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      With Lennon feeling that the chorus was missing something, Preston and Evans
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rodriguez_p_21_15-3">
       <a href="#cite_note-Rodriguez_p_21-15">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        15
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      were sent to bring in a group of people from a nightclub to provide backing vocals.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Listen52_48-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Listen52-48">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        46
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      These newcomers and all the musicians, along with Allen Klein, then added chorus vocals,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-C.26P_p_171_46-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-C.26P_p_171-46">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        45
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      with Harrison directing the singing.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Spizer_p_28_32-4">
       <a href="#cite_note-Spizer_p_28-32">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        31
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Although Lennon and Spector disagreed over the bass sound,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Listen51_37-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Listen51-37">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        36
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Lennon was "ecstatic" about the producer's work on "Instant Karma", author Peter Doggett writes.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Doggett_p_115_39-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Doggett_p_115-39">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        38
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      White's drums assumed the role of a lead instrument,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49">
       <a href="#cite_note-49">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        47
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      positioned prominently in the mix,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Brown_p_242_44-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-Brown_p_242-44">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        43
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      of which Spector biographer
      <a href="/wiki/Richard_Williams_(journalist)" title="Richard Williams (journalist)">
       Richard Williams
      </a>
      would write in 1972: "No Beatles record had ever possessed such a unique sound; Spector had used echo to make the drums reverberate like someone slapping a wet fish on a marble slab, and the voices sounded hollow and decayed."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schaffner_p_138_50-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Schaffner_p_138-50">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        48
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Spector wanted to add a
      <a href="/wiki/String_section" title="String section">
       string section
      </a>
      to the track in Los Angeles, but Lennon insisted that the recording was complete.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Brown_p_242_44-3">
       <a href="#cite_note-Brown_p_242-44">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        43
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ribowsky_p_252_45-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-Ribowsky_p_252-45">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        44
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Having only recently returned to producing, after the commercial failure of
      <a href="/wiki/Ike_%26_Tina_Turner" title="Ike &amp; Tina Turner">
       Ike &amp; Tina Turner
      </a>
      's 1966 single "
      <a href="/wiki/River_Deep_%E2%80%93_Mountain_High" title="River Deep  Mountain High">
       River Deep  Mountain High
      </a>
      " in America,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51">
       <a href="#cite_note-51">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        49
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Spector had "passed the audition", according to
      <i>
       Beatles Forever
      </i>
      author
      <a href="/wiki/Nicholas_Schaffner" title="Nicholas Schaffner">
       Nicholas Schaffner
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schaffner_p_138_50-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Schaffner_p_138-50">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        48
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      "Instant Karma!" was the first of many Beatles-related recordings that Spector worked on during the early 1970s,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JLBio133_21-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-JLBio133-21">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        20
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52">
       <a href="#cite_note-52">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        50
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      starting with the band's final album release,
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Let_It_Be" title="Let It Be">
        Let It Be
       </a>
      </i>
      (1970).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schaffner_p_138_50-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-Schaffner_p_138-50">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        48
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Brown_p_243_53-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Brown_p_243-53">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        51
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56">
       <a href="#cite_note-56">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        nb 3
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id=".22Who_Has_Seen_the_Wind.3F.22">
       "Who Has Seen the Wind?"
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Instant_Karma!&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title='Edit section: "Who Has Seen the Wind?"'>
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      As with the Plastic Ono Band's previous singles, "Give Peace a Chance" and "
      <a href="/wiki/Cold_Turkey" title="Cold Turkey">
       Cold Turkey
      </a>
      ", Lennon and Ono recorded an Ono composition as a
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/B-side" title="B-side">
       B-side
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57">
       <a href="#cite_note-57">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        54
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Produced by Lennon,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58">
       <a href="#cite_note-58">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        55
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      "Who Has Seen the Wind?" was recorded at
      <a href="/wiki/Trident_Studios" title="Trident Studios">
       Trident Studios
      </a>
      in central London, also in late January 1970.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Listen52_48-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Listen52-48">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        46
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The opening verse, sung
      <a href="/wiki/A_cappella" title="A cappella">
       a cappella
      </a>
      by Ono, is from a work by nineteenth-century English poet
      <a href="/wiki/Christina_Rossetti" title="Christina Rossetti">
       Christina Rossetti
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Spizer_p_28_32-5">
       <a href="#cite_note-Spizer_p_28-32">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        31
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The instrumentation on the recording includes Lennon playing acoustic guitar;
      <a href="/wiki/John_Barham" title="John Barham">
       John Barham
      </a>
      , Harrison's regular collaborator and arranger,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59">
       <a href="#cite_note-59">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        56
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ZigZag_60-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-ZigZag-60">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        57
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      on
      <a href="/wiki/Harpsichord" title="Harpsichord">
       harpsichord
      </a>
      ; Ono on flute; and various
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Percussion_instruments" title="Percussion instruments">
       percussion instruments
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Spizer_p_28_32-6">
       <a href="#cite_note-Spizer_p_28-32">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        31
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Author
      <a href="/wiki/Bruce_Spizer" title="Bruce Spizer">
       Bruce Spizer
      </a>
      suggests that Harrison may also have participated, on acoustic guitar.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Spizer_p_28_32-7">
       <a href="#cite_note-Spizer_p_28-32">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        31
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Release">
       Release
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Instant_Karma!&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Release">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <p>
      "Instant Karma!" ranks as one of the fastest-released songs in pop music history,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HIST_61-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-HIST-61">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        58
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      arriving in UK record stores just ten days after it was written.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stories31_62-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Stories31-62">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        59
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Lennon remarked to the press that he "wrote it for breakfast, recorded it for lunch, and we're putting it out for dinner".
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HIST_61-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-HIST-61">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        58
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <a href="/wiki/Apple_Records" title="Apple Records">
       Apple Records
      </a>
      issued the single on 6 February 1970 in Britain  credited to the Plastic Ono Band  and on 20 February in America, where the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/A-side" title="A-side">
       A-side
      </a>
      was retitled "Instant Karma! (We All Shine On)" and credited to John Ono Lennon.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Listen45_63-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Listen45-63">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        60
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65">
       <a href="#cite_note-65">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        nb 4
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Spector remixed "Instant Karma!" for the US release without Lennon's knowledge.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ribowsky_p_252_45-3">
       <a href="#cite_note-Ribowsky_p_252-45">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        44
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66">
       <a href="#cite_note-66">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        62
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      As with "Cold Turkey", the single's standard Apple Records A-side face label carried the words "PLAY LOUD", in both the UK
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Listen45_63-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Listen45-63">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        60
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pobKarma_67-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-pobKarma-67">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        63
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      and America.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Spizer_pp_28-29_68-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Spizer_pp_28-29-68">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        64
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Reflecting the tender sound of "Who Has Seen the Wind?", the B-side label read "PLAY QUIET"
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Listen45_63-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-Listen45-63">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        60
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      (or "PLAY SOFT" in the US).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pobKarma_67-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-pobKarma-67">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        63
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Spizer_pp_28-29_68-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Spizer_pp_28-29-68">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        64
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The front of the US picture sleeve featured a black-and-white photo of Lennon along with a prominent producer's credit for Spector, while the reverse had a similar picture of Ono.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Spizer_pp_28-29_68-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-Spizer_pp_28-29-68">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        64
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Promotion">
       Promotion
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Instant_Karma!&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Promotion">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      Following a year of highly publicised peace campaigning by the Lennons in 1969, Apple press officer
      <a href="/wiki/Derek_Taylor" title="Derek Taylor">
       Derek Taylor
      </a>
      was concerned that they had exhausted the media's interest in their causes.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69">
       <a href="#cite_note-69">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        65
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      On 4 February 1970, Lennon and Ono donated a large plastic bag full of their hair, along with Apple's poster for the new single,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Woffinden_p_39_6-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Woffinden_p_39-6">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        6
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      to north London-based
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Black_power" title="Black power">
       black power
      </a>
      activist
      <a href="/wiki/Michael_X" title="Michael X">
       Michael X
      </a>
      , in return for a pair of
      <a href="/wiki/Muhammad_Ali" title="Muhammad Ali">
       Muhammad Ali
      </a>
      's bloodstained boxing shorts.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Norman_p_636_70-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Norman_p_636-70">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        66
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Miles_p_368_71-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Miles_p_368-71">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        67
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The "final proof" of the Lennons' "overexpose[ure]", according to Taylor, was that there was a large press turnout for the event yet "nobody printed anything".
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Doggett_p_118_8-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Doggett_p_118-8">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        8
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73">
       <a href="#cite_note-73">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        nb 5
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      On 11 February, Lennon and Ono filmed an appearance on
      <a href="/wiki/BBC_Television" title="BBC Television">
       BBC Television
      </a>
      's
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Top_of_the_Pops" title="Top of the Pops">
        Top of the Pops
       </a>
      </i>
      to promote "Instant Karma!",
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stories31_62-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Stories31-62">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        59
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      accompanied by White, Voormann, Evans and
      <a href="/wiki/BP_Fallon" title="BP Fallon">
       BP Fallon
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Listen50_10-3">
       <a href="#cite_note-Listen50-10">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        10
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      While the other musicians mimed their contributions, Lennon sang a live vocal over a mix of the song's instrumental track,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Listen50_10-4">
       <a href="#cite_note-Listen50-10">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        10
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      prepared by EMI engineer
      <a href="/wiki/Geoff_Emerick" title="Geoff Emerick">
       Geoff Emerick
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Madinger_.26_Easter_p_34_74-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Madinger_.26_Easter_p_34-74">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        69
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      This was the first appearance on the program by any member of the Beatles since 1966,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Listen50_10-5">
       <a href="#cite_note-Listen50-10">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        10
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      as well as the public unveiling of the Lennons' new cropped look.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schaffner_p_137_30-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Schaffner_p_137-30">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        29
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Two versions of "Instant Karma!"  known as "cue card" and "knitting"  were taped for
      <i>
       Top of the Pops
      </i>
      , and aired on 12 and 19 February, respectively.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Listen50_10-6">
       <a href="#cite_note-Listen50-10">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        10
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      In the "cue card" clip, Lennon is wearing a flower-pattern shirt underneath a denim jacket, while Ono holds up a series of cryptically worded
      <a href="/wiki/Cue_card" title="Cue card">
       cue cards
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Madinger_.26_Easter_p_34_74-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Madinger_.26_Easter_p_34-74">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        69
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75">
       <a href="#cite_note-75">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        nb 6
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      In the "knitting" clip, Lennon wears a black
      <a href="/wiki/Polo_neck" title="Polo neck">
       polo-neck
      </a>
      jumper as Ono sits beside his piano,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Madinger_.26_Easter_p_34_74-3">
       <a href="#cite_note-Madinger_.26_Easter_p_34-74">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        69
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      blindfolded, and knitting throughout.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76">
       <a href="#cite_note-76">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        70
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Commercial_success_and_aftermath">
       Commercial success and aftermath
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Instant_Karma!&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Commercial success and aftermath">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      "Instant Karma!" was commercially successful,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77">
       <a href="#cite_note-77">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        71
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      peaking at number 3 on America's
      <a href="/wiki/Billboard_Hot_100" title="Billboard Hot 100">
       <i>
        Billboard
       </i>
       Hot 100
      </a>
      chart,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bill_78-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Bill-78">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        72
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      number 2 in Canada,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RPM_79-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-RPM-79">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        73
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      and number 5 on the
      <a href="/wiki/UK_Singles_Chart" title="UK Singles Chart">
       UK Singles Chart
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OCC_80-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-OCC-80">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        74
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83">
       <a href="#cite_note-83">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        nb 7
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The single also reached the top ten in several other European countries
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ultra_84-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Ultra-84">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        77
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      and in Australia.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auschart_85-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-auschart-85">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        78
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The release took place two months before
      <a href="/wiki/Paul_McCartney" title="Paul McCartney">
       Paul McCartney
      </a>
      announced the
      <a href="/wiki/Break-up_of_the_Beatles" title="Break-up of the Beatles">
       break-up
      </a>
      of the Beatles,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Listen45_63-3">
       <a href="#cite_note-Listen45-63">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        60
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      whose penultimate single, the
      <a href="/wiki/George_Martin" title="George Martin">
       George Martin
      </a>
      -produced "
      <a href="/wiki/Let_It_Be_(song)" title="Let It Be (song)">
       Let It Be
      </a>
      ", Lennon's record competed with on the US charts.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Spizer_p_27_81-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Spizer_p_27-81">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        75
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      "Instant Karma!" went on to become the first single by a solo Beatle to achieve US sales of 1 million,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Norman_p_635_13-4">
       <a href="#cite_note-Norman_p_635-13">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        13
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      earning
      <a href="/wiki/Music_recording_sales_certification" title="Music recording sales certification">
       gold
      </a>
      certification by the
      <a href="/wiki/Recording_Industry_Association_of_America" title="Recording Industry Association of America">
       Recording Industry Association of America
      </a>
      on 14 December 1970.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RIAA_86-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-RIAA-86">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        79
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-C.26P_p_332_87-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-C.26P_p_332-87">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        80
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89">
       <a href="#cite_note-89">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        nb 8
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Until
      <a href="/wiki/Death_of_John_Lennon" title="Death of John Lennon">
       Lennon's death
      </a>
      in December 1980, "Instant Karma!" remained his sole RIAA-certified gold single.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RIAA_86-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-RIAA-86">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        79
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Despite the stated intentions for Lennon and Ono's Year 1 AP, the proceeds from the auctioning of their hair benefited Michael X's Black House commune
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Norman_p_636_70-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Norman_p_636-70">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        66
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      rather than the peace movement,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Woffinden_p_39_6-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-Woffinden_p_39-6">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        6
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90">
       <a href="#cite_note-90">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        82
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      and, in the words of
      <i>
       Beatles Diary
      </i>
      author
      <a href="/wiki/Barry_Miles" title="Barry Miles">
       Barry Miles
      </a>
      , the pledge to donate their royalties was also "discreetly forgotten".
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91">
       <a href="#cite_note-91">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        83
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      In March 1970, Lennon publicly split with the organisers of the planned Toronto Peace Festival,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92">
       <a href="#cite_note-92">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        84
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      as he and Ono began treatment under
      <a href="/wiki/Arthur_Janov" title="Arthur Janov">
       Arthur Janov
      </a>
      's
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Primal_Therapy" title="Primal Therapy">
       Primal Therapy
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93">
       <a href="#cite_note-93">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        85
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94">
       <a href="#cite_note-94">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        86
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Before heading to California in April for intensive therapy through the summer,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95">
       <a href="#cite_note-95">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        87
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96">
       <a href="#cite_note-96">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        88
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Lennon accused McCartney of using the Beatles' break-up to sell his album
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/McCartney_(album)" title="McCartney (album)">
        McCartney
       </a>
      </i>
      ,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97">
       <a href="#cite_note-97">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        89
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      and admitted that he wished that he himself had announced the break-up months before to promote his own solo release.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98">
       <a href="#cite_note-98">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        90
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99">
       <a href="#cite_note-99">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        91
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Critical_reception">
       Critical reception
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Instant_Karma!&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Critical reception">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <p>
      On release,
      <a href="/wiki/Chris_Welch" title="Chris Welch">
       Chris Welch
      </a>
      of
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Melody_Maker" title="Melody Maker">
        Melody Maker
       </a>
      </i>
      declared: "Instant hit! John Lennon is singing better than ever. With a beautiful rock 'n' roll echo chamber on his mean but meaningful vocals and some superb drumming, it makes up the Plastics' best piece of boogie yet."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100">
       <a href="#cite_note-100">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        92
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Writing in the
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/NME" title="NME">
        NME
       </a>
      </i>
      in 1975,
      <a href="/wiki/Charles_Shaar_Murray" title="Charles Shaar Murray">
       Charles Shaar Murray
      </a>
      wrote of the song's "volcanically desperate optimism" and rated it "a classic". Shaar Murray added, with reference to "Cold Turkey" also: "I can't remember anybody else who put out two such utter killers in a row over the same period of time."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101">
       <a href="#cite_note-101">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        93
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102">
       <a href="#cite_note-102">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        94
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      <i>
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Village_Voice" title="Village Voice">
        Village Voice
       </a>
      </i>
      critic
      <a href="/wiki/Robert_Christgau" title="Robert Christgau">
       Robert Christgau
      </a>
      has described "Instant Karma!" as Lennon's "best political song",
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103">
       <a href="#cite_note-103">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        95
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      while other reviewers consider it his finest post-Beatles recording.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104">
       <a href="#cite_note-104">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        96
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      In their 1975 book
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/The_Beatles:_An_Illustrated_Record" title="The Beatles: An Illustrated Record">
        The Beatles: An Illustrated Record
       </a>
      </i>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Roy_Carr" title="Roy Carr">
       Roy Carr
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Tony_Tyler" title="Tony Tyler">
       Tony Tyler
      </a>
      describe "Instant Karma!" as a "snappy little rocker" that "owes as much to the skilful production of Phil Spector as to the vitality of the overall performance", on which "[d]rummer Alan White excels."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Carr_.26_Tyler_p_86_43-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Carr_.26_Tyler_p_86-43">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        42
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Carr and Tyler remark that "Who Has Seen the Wind?" "would have made a marvellous soundtrack for the movie of
      <a href="/wiki/Henry_James" title="Henry James">
       Henry James
      </a>
      ' '
      <a href="/wiki/The_Turn_of_the_Screw" title="The Turn of the Screw">
       The Turn of the Screw
      </a>
      ', being a somewhat sinister ditty sung
      <i>
        la
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wunderkind" title="Wunderkind">
        Wunderkind
       </a>
      </i>
      ".
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Carr_.26_Tyler_p_86_43-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-Carr_.26_Tyler_p_86-43">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        42
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Bruce Spizer describes Ono's song as "evok[ing] images of minstrels at a
      <a href="/wiki/Renaissance_fair" title="Renaissance fair">
       Renaissance fair
      </a>
      " and considers the single "a far cry  and welcome relief from the
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Avant-garde" title="Avant-garde">
        avant-garde
       </a>
      </i>
      discs issued by John and Yoko in 1969".
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105">
       <a href="#cite_note-105">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        97
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Another to highlight White's drumming amid the "collective genius" of all the participants on "Instant Karma!", author Robert Rodriguez concludes of Lennon's activities on 27 January 1970: "Not many days in the history of rock and roll proved as everlastingly fruitful."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rodriguez_p_21_15-4">
       <a href="#cite_note-Rodriguez_p_21-15">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        15
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      In 1981,
      <i>
       NME
      </i>
      critic
      <a href="/wiki/Bob_Woffinden" title="Bob Woffinden">
       Bob Woffinden
      </a>
      wrote of the single: "It was excellent. Lennon was characteristically simple and direct, but this time on a song with one of those magically catchy refrains."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Woffinden_p_32_106-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Woffinden_p_32-106">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        98
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Among Lennon's biographers, Ben Urish and Kenneth Bielen view "Instant Karma!" as "a chiding though positive message for humanity",
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-W.26MJL16_24-3">
       <a href="#cite_note-W.26MJL16-24">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        23
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      while
      <a href="/wiki/Jon_Wiener" title="Jon Wiener">
       Jon Wiener
      </a>
      praises Lennon's "rich, deep voice" on a recording where the sound is "irresistible".
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107">
       <a href="#cite_note-107">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        99
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Philip Norman describes the song as "similar to 'Cold Turkey' in
      <a href="/wiki/Tempo" title="Tempo">
       tempo
      </a>
      but far more relaxed and humorous", adding that Spector's production gave Lennon's voice "a taut expressiveness it had not had since '
      <a href="/wiki/Norwegian_Wood_(This_Bird_Has_Flown)" title="Norwegian Wood (This Bird Has Flown)">
       Norwegian Wood
      </a>
      '".
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Norman_p_635_13-5">
       <a href="#cite_note-Norman_p_635-13">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        13
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      While noting the significance of the session for George Harrison's career, author Simon Leng praises the recording as being "full of urgency and sheer excitement".
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Leng_p_70_40-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Leng_p_70-40">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        39
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      In 1989,
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Rolling_Stone" title="Rolling Stone">
        Rolling Stone
       </a>
      </i>
      magazine ranked "Instant Karma!" the 79th best single of the previous 25 years.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108">
       <a href="#cite_note-108">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        100
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      In
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/NME" title="NME">
        NME Originals
       </a>
       : Beatles  The Solo Years 19701980
      </i>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/David_Stubbs" title="David Stubbs">
       David Stubbs
      </a>
      lists the song second among Lennon's "ten solo gems" (behind "Cold Turkey"), with the comment: "'Instant Karma!' epitomises the Lennon paradox, melding hippie idealism and rock'n'roll primal energy in an exhilarating mix."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109">
       <a href="#cite_note-109">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        101
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Matt Melis of
      <a href="/wiki/Consequence_of_Sound" title="Consequence of Sound">
       Consequence of Sound
      </a>
      placed it third on his 2009 list of "Top Ten Songs by Ex-Beatles".
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110">
       <a href="#cite_note-110">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        102
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      According to the website
      <a href="/wiki/Acclaimed_Music" title="Acclaimed Music">
       Acclaimed Music
      </a>
      , "Instant Karma!" has also appeared in the following critics' best-songs lists and books, among others:
      <a href="/wiki/Dave_Marsh" title="Dave Marsh">
       Dave Marsh
      </a>
      's
      <i>
       The 1001 Greatest Singles Ever Made
      </i>
      (1989; at number 638),
      <i>
       1000 Songs that Rock Your World
      </i>
      by
      <a href="/wiki/Dave_Thompson_(author)" title="Dave Thompson (author)">
       Dave Thompson
      </a>
      (2011; number 56), the
      <i>
       NME
      </i>
      's "The 100 Best Songs of the 1970s" (2012; number 77), and
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Q_(magazine)" title="Q (magazine)">
        Q
       </a>
      </i>
      magazine's "The 1001 Best Songs Ever" (2003; number 193).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111">
       <a href="#cite_note-111">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        103
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Re-releases_and_live_version">
       Re-releases and live version
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Instant_Karma!&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Re-releases and live version">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <p>
      "Instant Karma!"'s first appearance on a Lennon album, albeit slightly edited in length, was the 1975 compilation
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Shaved_Fish" title="Shaved Fish">
        Shaved Fish
       </a>
      </i>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pobKarma_67-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-pobKarma-67">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        63
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Urish and Bielen observe that the "advertising hyperbole" inherent in the song's title, through the inclusion of an exclamation mark, is given extra emphasis on this album cover.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-W.26MJL16_24-4">
       <a href="#cite_note-W.26MJL16-24">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        23
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113">
       <a href="#cite_note-113">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        nb 9
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The song has featured  often with the full title "Instant Karma! (We All Shine On)"  on numerous posthumous compilations, including
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/The_John_Lennon_Collection" title="The John Lennon Collection">
        The John Lennon Collection
       </a>
      </i>
      (1982), the
      <i>
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lennon_(album)" title="Lennon (album)">
        Lennon
       </a>
      </i>
      box set (1990),
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Lennon_Legend:_The_Very_Best_of_John_Lennon" title="Lennon Legend: The Very Best of John Lennon">
        Lennon Legend
       </a>
      </i>
      (1997),
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114">
       <a href="#cite_note-114">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        105
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Working_Class_Hero:_The_Definitive_Lennon" title="Working Class Hero: The Definitive Lennon">
        Working Class Hero: The Definitive Lennon
       </a>
      </i>
      (2005) and
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Power_to_the_People:_The_Hits" title="Power to the People: The Hits">
        Power to the People: The Hits
       </a>
      </i>
      (2010).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115">
       <a href="#cite_note-115">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        106
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      "Who Has Seen the Wind?" appeared as a bonus track on the 1997
      <a href="/wiki/Rykodisc" title="Rykodisc">
       Rykodisc
      </a>
      reissue of the couple's third album of
      <a href="/wiki/Experimental_music" title="Experimental music">
       experimental music
      </a>
      ,
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Wedding_Album" title="Wedding Album">
        Wedding Album
       </a>
      </i>
      (1969).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116">
       <a href="#cite_note-116">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        107
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Lennon played "Instant Karma!" at his last full-length concert performance
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117">
       <a href="#cite_note-117">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        108
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
       the One to One benefit shows held at
      <a href="/wiki/Madison_Square_Garden" title="Madison Square Garden">
       Madison Square Garden
      </a>
      , New York, on 30 August 1972.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118">
       <a href="#cite_note-118">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        109
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      His backing band comprised the group
      <a href="/wiki/Elephant%27s_Memory" title="Elephant's Memory">
       Elephant's Memory
      </a>
      ,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119">
       <a href="#cite_note-119">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        110
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      in addition to Ono and drummer
      <a href="/wiki/Jim_Keltner" title="Jim Keltner">
       Jim Keltner
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120">
       <a href="#cite_note-120">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        111
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The 1986 album and video
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Live_in_New_York_City_(John_Lennon_album)" title="Live in New York City (John Lennon album)">
        Live in New York City
       </a>
      </i>
      contains the afternoon performance of the song.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121">
       <a href="#cite_note-121">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        112
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      In July 1992, "Instant Karma!" was re-released as a single in the Netherlands, backed by "
      <a href="/wiki/Oh_My_Love" title="Oh My Love">
       Oh My Love
      </a>
      ".
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Listen50_10-7">
       <a href="#cite_note-Listen50-10">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        10
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Originally, copies of it were given away with early editions of
      <i>
       The John Lennon Video Colllection
      </i>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Listen50_10-8">
       <a href="#cite_note-Listen50-10">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        10
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      When released in the rest of Europe (barring the UK), this single reissue gained two extra B-sides: "
      <a href="/wiki/Mother_(John_Lennon_song)" title="Mother (John Lennon song)">
       Mother
      </a>
      " and "Bless You".
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Listen50_10-9">
       <a href="#cite_note-Listen50-10">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        10
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Of the two 1970
      <i>
       Top of the Pops
      </i>
      performances, the "cue card" version appeared on
      <i>
       The John Lennon Video Collection
      </i>
      in October 1992,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122">
       <a href="#cite_note-122">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        113
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      while the "knitting" performance was remixed and extended for release on the
      <a href="/wiki/Lennon_Legend:_The_Very_Best_of_John_Lennon_(DVD)" title="Lennon Legend: The Very Best of John Lennon (DVD)">
       <i>
        Lennon Legend
       </i>
       DVD
      </a>
      (2003).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Listen50_10-10">
       <a href="#cite_note-Listen50-10">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        10
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The "knitting" version was also included on the 2003 UK single "
